                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Developing an O3 type layered oxide cathode and its application in 18650 commercial type Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tripathi, Abhinav; Rudola, Ashish; Gajjela, Satyanarayana Reddy; Xi, Shibo; Balaya, Palani</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">45</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">25944-25960</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel, water-stable and high energy d. cathode material Na0.9Cu0.12Ni0.10Fe0.30Mn0.43Ti0.05O2 (NCNFMT) is reported here along with a thorough understanding of structural events during battery operation.  Systematic substitutions are carried out, which lead to increase in specific energy densities of this family of cathodes from 274.6 W h kgcathode-1 (NCFM - Na0.9Cu0.22Fe0.30Mn0.48O2) to 304.2 W h kgcathode-1 (NCFMT - Na0.9Cu0.22Fe0.30Mn0.43Ti0.05O2) and finally to 350.7 W h kgcathode-1 (NCNFMT - Na0.9Cu0.12Ni0.10Fe0.30Mn0.43Ti0.05O2).  Operando X-ray diffraction reveals phase transformations and ex situ EXAFS shows the evolution of local environments around transition metals during charge/discharge.  Monoclinic distortions in the NCFM material during O3-P3 phase transformations are suppressed by Ti4+ substitution leading to improvements in the cycling performance of NCFMT.  Cu-O octahedral sites exhibit huge Jahn-Teller distortion: Ni2+ substitution in place of Cu2+ not only leads to more ordered Ni-O, but it also helps ext. more Na ions from the O3 cathode structure, thus boosting the capacity while also showing good cycling stability due to the highly reversible bond-length and local environmental changes as revealed by EXAFS analyses.  XPS shows a titanium-rich surface for NCFMT and NCNFMT which helps improve water-stability.  The capacity retention after 200 cycles at 0.2C is 84%, 96% and 90% for NCFM, NCFMT and NCNFMT resp.  The delivered storage capacities of NCFM, NCFMT and NCNFMT are 21 mA h g-1, 47 mA h g-1 and 60 mA h g-1 resp. at 3C. 18650 type Na-ion batteries using the NCNFMT cathode material against a hard carbon anode are also reported to demonstrate potential scalability of the NCNFMT cathode and efficacy of a 1 M NaBF4 tetraglyme electrolyte system for the first time. 18650 cells deliver a specific energy d. of 62 W h kgtotal_18650_weight-1 with 90% energy efficiency, thus being suitable for large scale energy storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/ange.201606508"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goodenough, J.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Performance of NASICON symmetric cell with ionic liquid electrolyte</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Plashnitsa, Larisa S.; Kobayashi, Eiji; Noguchi, Yoshinori; Okada, Shigeto; Yamaki, Jin-ichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To improve the thermal stability of lithium- and sodium-ion batteries, the room-temp. molten salts LiBF4/1-ethyl-3-Me imidazolium tetrafluoroborate (EMIBF4) and NaBF4/EMIBF4 were used as ionic liq. (IL) electrolytes instead of flammable carbonate-type org. electrolyte solvents.  To avoid cathodic decompn. of the IL electrolytes, a sym. cell configuration with Na Superionic CONductor (NASICON)-type A3V2(PO4)3 (where A is Li or Na) as both cathode and anode was tried in a coin-type cell (type 2320).  As a result, both the polyanionic-based Li3V2(PO4)3 (LVP) as well as Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) sym. cells using org. electrolytes were found to operate as secondary batteries and exhibited satisfactory electrochem. performances.  The substitution of the org. electrolytes by the appropriate IL electrolytes in both cases resulted in the redn. in the first discharge capacities.  However, the IL-based cells revealed better cyclability and a more stable behavior at elevated temps.  The obtained electrochem. behavior of the sym. cells was confirmed by the complex impedance measurements at 25 and 80°C.  In addn., the thermal stability of LVP and NVP with the IL electrolytes was also examd.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/er.7771"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Moossa, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">James Abraham, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kahraman, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Al-Qaradawi, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Al Tahtamouni, T.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Bipolar Electrodes for Next-Generation Rechargeable Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Tiefeng; Yuan, Yifei; Tao, Xinyong; Lin, Zhan; Lu, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Science (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2001207</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ASDCCF</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The development of advanced rechargeable batteries provides a great opportunity for basic and applied researchers to collectively overcome challenging scientific and technol. barriers that directly address a crit. need for energy storage.  In addn. to novel battery chemistries often scientifically reviewed, advanced battery structures via technol. innovations that boost battery performance are also worthy of attention.  In this context, bipolar electrodes (BEs) are capable of improving the specific power, simplifying cell components, and reducing manufg. costs for rechargeable batteries.  By focusing on the fundamentals and applications of BEs in rechargeable batteries, the rational utilization of BEs from an academic perspective is considered.  The progress and challenges of BEs are discussed and summarized in detail.  Key techniques and materials for enabling BEs are highlighted and an outlook for the future directions of BEs that involve emerging concepts, such as wearable devices, all-solid-state batteries, fast spraying fabrication, and recyclable secondary batteries, is also presented.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201501527"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Advances and Prospects of Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>27</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">36</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">5343</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">5364</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adma.201501527</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D27%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D5343-5364%26journal%3DAdv.%2BMater.%26issue%3D36%26author%3DX.%2BXiang%26author%3DK.%2BZhang%26author%3DJ.%2BChen%26title%3DRecent%2BAdvances%2Band%2BProspects%2Bof%2BCathode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadma.201501527&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c10189&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadma.201501527&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances and Prospects of Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiang, Xingde; Zhang, Kai; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">36</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5343-5364</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Na-ion batteries (SIBs) receive significant attention for electrochem. energy storage and conversion owing to their wide availability and the low cost of Na resources.  However, SIBs face challenges of low specific energy, short cycling life, and insufficient specific power, owing to the heavy mass and large radius of Na+ ions.  As an important component of SIBs, cathode materials have a significant effect on the SIB electrochem. performance.  The most recent advances and prospects of inorg. and org. cathode materials are summarized here.  Among current cathode materials, layered transition-metal oxides achieve high specific energies around 600 mW h g-1 owing to their high specific capacities of 180-220 mA h g-1 and their moderate operating potentials of 2.7-3.2 V (vs. Na+/Na).  Porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C nanomaterials exhibit excellent cycling performance with almost 100% retention over 1000 cycles owing to their robust structural framework.  Recent emerging cathode materials, such as amorphous NaFePO4 and pteridine derivs. show interesting electrochem. properties and attractive prospects for application in SIBs.  Future work should focus on strategies to enhance the overall performance of cathode materials in terms of specific energy, cycling life, and rate capability with cationic doping, anionic substitution, morphol. fabrication, and electrolyte matching.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200803"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saravanan, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mason, C. W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rudola, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wong, K.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The first report on excellent cycling stability and superior rate capability of Na3V2(PO4)3 for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Saravanan, Kuppan; Mason, Chad W.; Rudola, Ashish; Wong, Kim Hai; Balaya, Palani</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">444-450</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries are attractive for the rapidly emerging large-scale energy storage market for intermittent renewable resources.  Currently a viable cathode material does not exist for practical non-aq. sodium ion battery applications.  Here we disclose a high performance, durable electrode material based on the 3D NASICON framework.  Porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C was synthesized using a novel soln.-based approach.  This material, as a cathode, is capable of delivering an energy storage capacity of ∼400 mWh/g vs. sodium metal.  Furthermore, at high current rates (10, 20 and 40 C), it displayed remarkable capacity retention.  Equally impressive is the long term cycle life.  Nearly 50% of the initial capacity was retained after 30,000 charge/discharge cycles at 40 C (4.7 A/g).  Notably, coulombic efficiency was 99.68% (av.) over the course of cycling.  To the best of our knowledge, the combination of high energy d., high power d. and ultra long cycle life demonstrated here has never been reported before for sodium ion batteries.  We believe our findings will have profound implications for developing large-scale energy storage systems for renewable energy sources.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202000473"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Engineering of Polyanion Type Cathode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries: Toward Higher Energy/Power Density</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Poly(vinylidene fluoride)-based, co-polymer separator electrolyte membranes for lithium-ion battery systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Costa, C. M.; Gomez Ribelles, J. L.; Lanceros-Mendez, S.; Appetecchi, G. B.; Scrosati, B.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">245</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors report and discuss the physicochem. properties of novel electrolyte membranes, based on poly(vinylidenefluoride-co-trifluoroethylene), PVdF-TrFE, and poly(vinylidenefluoride-co-hexafluoropropylene), PVdF-HFP, co-polymer hosts and the PVdF-TrFE/poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO) blend as separators for Li battery systems.  The examd. separator membranes, particularly those based on the PVdF co-polymers, are able to uptake large liq. amts. leading to high ionic cond. values.  Tests performed on Li/LiFePO4 and Li/Sn-C cells revealed very good cycling performance even at high current rates and 100% of DOD, approaching the results achieved in liq. electrolytes.  A capacity fading &lt;0.002% per cycle was obsd.  Particularly, the Li/LiFePO4 cathode cells have exhibited excellent rate capability, being still able to deliver at 2 C &gt;89% of the capacity discharged at 0.1 C.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical stability of ether based salt-in-polymer based electrolytes: Computational investigation of the effect of substitution and the type of salt</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pandian, S.; Adiga, S. P.; Tagade, P.; Hariharan, K. S.; Mayya, K. S.; Lee, Y.-G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">393</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. stability window (EW) of polyether based salt-in-polymer electrolytes was investigated using d. functional theory (DFT) calcns.  The electrolyte systems investigated consisted of polyethylene oxide (PEO) in either lithium-bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (LiTFSI) or lithium-hexafluorophosphate (LiPF6) salt and the EW was detd. by performing calcns. of redn. and oxidn. potentials of isolated ethylene oxide (EO) oligomer and the resp. salt species in a continuum solvent.  The simulations suggest that the cathodic limit of the polymer-salt system is defined by the redn. potential of the salt anion and that both salt anions considered are unstable against Li anode.  The anodic limit is defined by EO and it is stable against most com. cathodes.  Including explicit salt mols. in the calcns. shows that the predicted EW is changed by ∼0.4V.  The calcns. further reveal that the EW is dependent on the type of the salt mol.  Perfluoropolyether, a perfluorinated analog of PEO that has lower redn. potential and higher oxidn. potential in isolation as compared to PEO, improved both oxidn. and redn. stability of the polymer-salt system.  Substitution of other functional groups to PEO improved the electrochem. stability to potentially accommodate higher voltage window requirements.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref26" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1021/acsaem.3c02137"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Patra, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matsumi, N.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Charge–Discharge Behavior of Lithium-Ion Batteries Using a Polymer Electrolyte Bearing High-Density Functional Groups</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With higher nature abundance and lower prodn. cost of sodium-based materials compared to lithium-based ones, sodium batteries have been arising as one of the most promising energy storage systems serving as complementary power sources to the popular lithium-ion batteries.  Replacing conventional liq. electrolytes contg. flammable liq. solvents with polymer electrolytes (PEs) empowers sodium batteries with inherently enhanced safety at a low expense of processability.  In this contribution, we present a comprehensive overview on the research progresses and advances in the PE-based sodium batteries, including three main categories of PEs, i.e., solid polymer electrolytes (SPEs), composite solid polymer electrolytes (CSPEs), and plasticized/gel polymer electrolytes (PPEs/GPEs).  The energy densities of sodium batteries at cell level are estd. and compared with their lithium counterparts.  The research development of PE-based sodium batteries is scrutinized and the remaining challenges and possible solns. are intensively discussed.  The purpose of the present work is not only to provide a well-balanced status quo of PE-based sodium batteries but also to address the possible remedies for improving their performance and other post lithium battery technologies, in hope of facilitating their large-scale deployment in the market.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref28" class="refNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit28" data-doi="10.1021/acsaem.2c01296"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bristi, A. C.; Lanceros-Mendez, S.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Progress in Polymer Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Poly(vinylidene fluoride), PVDF, and its copolymers are the family of polymers with the highest dielec. const. and electroactive response, including piezoelec., pyroelec. and ferroelec. effects.  The electroactive properties are increasingly important in a wide range of applications such as in biomedicine, energy generation and storage, monitoring and control, and include the development of sensors and actuators, separator and filtration membranes and smart scaffolds, among others.  For many of these applications the polymer should be in one of its electroactive phases.  This review presents the developments and summarizes the main characteristics of the electroactive phases of PVDF and copolymers, indicates the different processing strategies as well as the way in which the phase content is identified and quantified.  Addnl., recent advances in the development of electroactive composites allowing novel effects, such as magnetoelec. responses, and opening new applications areas are presented.  Finally, some of the more interesting potential applications and processing challenges are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1039/C7RA01267E"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lei, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Critical Analysis of the α, β and γ Phases in Poly(Vinylidene Fluoride) Using FTIR</span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">25</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">15382</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">15389</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A critical analysis of the α,β and γ phases in poly(vinylidene fluoride) using FTIR</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cai, Xiaomei; Lei, Tingping; Sun, Daoheng; Lin, Liwei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) has been widely utilized in scientific research and the manufg. industry for its unique piezoelec. properties.  In the past few decades, the vibrational spectra of PVDF polymorphic polymers via FTIR (Fourier transform IR spectroscopy) have been extensively investigated and documented.  However, reports on the anal. of α,β and γ phases often have conflicting views based on measured data.  In this work, we analyze the FTIR vibrational bands of PVDF materials fabricated by different processes with detailed XRD (X-ray diffraction) characterization to identify the structural α,β and γ phases.  By examg. the results in this work and extensively reviewing published research reports in the literature, a universal phase identification procedure using only the FTIR results is proposed and validated.  Specifically, this procedure can differentiate the three phases by checking the bands around 763 and/or 614, 1275, and 1234 cm-1 for the α,β and γ phases, resp.                         cas_cit32R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">32</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Multiple stage crystallization of gamma phase poly(vinylidene fluoride) induced by ion-dipole interaction as revealed by time-resolved FTIR and two-dimensional correlation analysis</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yue; Xu, Jia-Zhuang; Zhu, Lei; Xu, Huan; Pan, Ming-Wang; Zhong, Gan-Ji; Li, Zhong-Ming</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Polymer</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The polymorphic γ poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) induced by an ionic surfactant, cetyltrimethyl ammonium bromide (CTAB), is studied by using time-resolved Fourier transform IR (FTIR) and two-dimensional (2D) correlation anal.  It is found that an extremely low content of CTAB can effectively induce γ PVDF due to the existence of strong ion-dipole interactions.  Five stages are identified and intensive information on the growth and phase transition of α and γ phases in each stage is offered.  Specifically, an induction periods obsd. for both α and γ phases in the stage I.  In stage II, the α phase is initiated while the γ phase is still in the induction period, i.e., the nucleation and development of α phase are prior to the γ phase, although the resultant sample presents exclusively pure γ phase.  The nucleation effect of CTAB for the γ phase is triggered in stage III, but α phase still grows faster than that of γ phase.  Incorporating more CTAB impedes the growth of α phase while accelerates the development of γ phase.  In stage IV, an α-γ phase solid transition accompanies the melt crystn. of the γ phase, and the α-γ phase transition is accelerated by CTAB mols. dramatically.  Finally, the melt crystn. of the γ phase is completed, only α-γ phase transition is obsd.  This effort indicates that the pure γ phase of PVDF induced by ion-dipole interactions could originate from multiple phase behaviors, which could be helpful for understanding and manipulation of PVDF polar phases.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref33" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2020.124916"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Polat, H. Gel-polymer electrolytes (GPEs) are now considered as the next-generation electrolytes to be used in Li-O2 systems.  PVDF-HFP polymers with different Li salts were successfully used as gel-polymer electrolytes to replace liq. electrolytes in Li-O2 batteries. The authors systematically obsd. the effect of different Li salts at various concns. on the above mentioned electrochem. properties.  The authors' results indicate that LiPF6 salt imparts best ionic cond. into GPE systems while LiTFSI has both the widest electrochem. window and highest Li-ion transference no.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Vibrational Properties of MBH4 and MBF4 Crystals (M = Li, Na, K): A Combined DFT, Infrared, and Raman Study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zavorotynska, Olena; Corno, Marta; Damin, Alessandro; Spoto, Giuseppe; Ugliengo, Piero; Baricco, Marcello</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Vibrational properties of alkali metal borohydrides and of the corresponding tetrafluoroborates are studied by comparing DFT harmonic vibrational IR and Raman spectra of the crystals with the exptl. ones, obtained by IR attenuated total reflection and Raman techniques.  The computed internal bending frequencies of the [BX4]- anions are in good agreement with the expt., and the computed stretching frequencies of tetrafluoroborates are slightly underestimated.  As expected, due to the neglecting of anharmonicity in the DFT spectra, the computed stretching frequencies of borohydrides are overestimated.  The peak assignment of the exptl. spectra is carried out in terms of factor group theory.  For borohydrides, it is mostly in agreement with previously published data but for a peak obsd. at ∼1400 cm-1.  The peak assignment for KBF4 and LiBF4 was carried out for the 1st time in terms of factor group theory.  This work is the 1st step on the way to detg. the vibrational properties of the MBH4 + MBF4 solid solns. for H storage materials with enhanced H2-release/uptake properties and solid-state electrolytes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref46" class="refNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit46" data-doi="10.1021/jp0271256"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chan, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Salts of the same anions of Mg2+, Na+, and Li+ exhibit different hygroscopic properties.  These differences are attributed to the mol. structural properties of the H bonding network of the H2O mols. in the 2nd and 1st hydrated layers of Mg2+, Na+, and Li+.  To study the structures of H2O mols., in particular, the presence of H2O monomers, Raman spectra of single levitated droplets of aq. NaClO4, LiClO4, and Mg(ClO4)2 solns. from dild. concns. to high supersaturations were measured.  Because heterogeneous nucleation was suppressed in these levitated droplets, supersatd. droplets with a H2O-to-solute ratio (WSR) ≥2 was achieved.  Taking advantage of the structure breaking effect of ClO-4on the H bonding network of H2O mols., Raman spectra of H2O monomers in these highly supersatd. droplets were obsd.  At a low WSR, two peaks at 3549 and 3588 cm-1 for H2O monomers with one and two weak H bonds with ClO4- were obsd. for NaClO4 droplets.  Compared with those of the NaClO4 droplets, the peaks of the H2O monomers in supersatd. LiClO4 and Mg(ClO4)2 droplets red shifted to 3553 and 3546 cm-1, resp.  This observation is consistent with the order of the increase of the polarization effect of the cations, i.e., Na+ &lt; Li+ &lt; Mg2+.  The intensity ratios of the strongly H-bonded components to the H2O monomers, i.e., I3440/I3549 and I3289/I3549 for NaClO4, I3455/I3553 and I3275/I3553 for LiClO4, and I3411/I3546 and I3440/I3549 for Mg(ClO4)2, were used to study the effects of the presence of ClO4- on the H2O structures of the hydration layers of Na+, Li+, and Mg2+.  The results were explained in terms of the stability of H2O mols. in the inner spheres of these hydrated cations.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref47" class="refNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit47" data-doi="10.1016/j.saa.2006.02.026"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Selvasekarapandian, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Baskaran, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kamishima, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kawamura, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hattori, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Laser Raman and FTIR Studies on Li+ Interaction in PVAc–LiClO4 Polymer Electrolytes</span>. <i>Spectrochim. Measured transport properties include the ionic cond. (σ), the differential salt diffusion coeff. (Ds), and the cationic transference no. (t0+).  To measure t0+, a recently developed technique based on concd. soln. theory is used.  Under this framework no assumptions need to be made concerning ideality of the electrolytic soln. Ds also varies less with complex compn. in concd. solns.  Interestingly, the cationic transference nos. are found to be neg. over a wide concn. range, i.e., t0- decreases with increasing salt concn. and reaches a low of -1.15 ± 0.25 at a compn. corresponding to an ether-oxygen to sodium-ion ratio of 7:1.                         cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Decoupling segmental relaxation and ionic conductivity for lithium-ion polymer electrolytes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Bresser, Dominic; Lyonnard, Sandrine; Iojoiu, Cristina; Picard, Lionel; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Molecular Systems Design &amp; Engineering</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">779-792</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The use of polymer electrolytes instead of liq. org. systems is considered key for enhancing the safety of lithium batteries and may, in addn., enable the transition to high-energy lithium metal anodes.  An intrinsic limitation, however, is their rather low ionic cond. at ambient temp.  Nonetheless, it has been suggested that this might be overcome by decoupling the ion transport and the segmental relaxation of the coordinating polymer.  Here, we provide an overview of the different approaches to achieve such decoupling, including a brief recapitulation of the segmental-relaxation dependent ion conduction mechanism, exemplarily focusing on the archetype of polymer electrolytes - polyethylene oxide (PEO).  In fact, while the understanding of the underlying mechanisms has greatly improved within recent years, it remains rather challenging to outperform PEO-based electrolyte systems.  Nonetheless, it is not impossible, as highlighted by several examples mentioned herein, esp. in consideration of the extremely rich polymer chem. and with respect to the substantial progress already achieved in designing tailored mols. with well-defined nanostructures.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref53" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2015.03.228"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Boschin, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Johansson, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Characterization of NaX (X: TFSI, FSI) – PEO Based Solid Polymer Electrolytes for Sodium Batteries</span>. <i>Electrochim.                         cas_cit53R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">53</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Characterization of NaX (X: TFSI, FSI) - PEO based solid polymer electrolytes for sodium batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Boschin, Andrea; Johansson, Patrik</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Solid polymers electrolytes (SPEs) based on Na bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide (NaFSI) and poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO) with different ether O to Na (O:Na) molar ratios (n), resulting in NaFSI(PEO)n materials are here presented for the 1st time.  These SPEs are extensively compared with the corresponding NaTFSI(PEO)n system in terms of ionic conductivities, thermal properties, and charge carriers - to in detail outline both the role of the different anions used and the salt concns. employed.  While for the most dil. systems (n = 20) the two SPE families show similar ionic conductivities in the entire temp. range studied (273-343 K), for n = 6 and n = 9 they differ significantly; at room temp., the NaFSI based SPEs show lower ionic conductivities than the NaTFSI based analogs.  This difference is mainly ascribed to differences in the morphol.; while the NaTFSI salt, possibly by virtue of its large TFSI anion, acts to inhibit crystn., NaFSI rather seems to favor crystn.  Also, careful Raman spectroscopy anal. of the charge carrier speciation reveal higher aggregates to be present in the most concd. SPE, NaFSI(PEO)6, and the NaFSI based SPEs in general to result in less free anions than the NaTFSI based SPEs.  Also, as both NaTFSI(PEO)n and NaFSI(PEO)n for n = 20 and n = 9 exhibit very similar glass transition temps., the FSI ion seem to be equally plasticizing as the TFSI ion, but for n = 6 the different speciation in terms of charge carriers also affects the relative dynamics of the polymer chains.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref54" class="refNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit54" data-doi="10.1107/S1600536810002801"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zatovsky, I.                         cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na3V2(PO4)3-A Highly Promising Anode and Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Akcay, Tolga; Haeringer, Marcel; Pfeifer, Kristina; Anhalt, Jens; Binder, Joachim R.; Dsoke, Sonia; Kramer, Dominik; Moenig, Reiner</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Energy Materials</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries may develop into a cost-efficient alternative to lithium-ion batteries.  Na3V2(PO4)3/C (NVP/C) is known to be a suitable electrode material for such batteries that can be used as an anode or cathode.  Here, NVP/C-based electrodes were investigated in different cell configurations.  The electrodes were cycled against the anode materials hard carbon, Sb/C, SnSb/C, and sodium metal.  Furthermore, NVP vs. NVP was investigated.  When NVP/C is cycled against the other anode materials, the cells exhibit relatively poor reliability, but in NVP-NVP cells, a high cycling stability was obsd. and more than 1500 cycles with a capacity retention of 80% were achieved.  This work demonstrates that common problems of Na and Na-ion cells result from the anode materials used and that NVP/C itself is very reliable both as an anode and cathode material.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref56" class="refNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit56" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-018-03324-7"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Banis, M.                         cas_cit57R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">57</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Phosphate Framework Electrode Materials for Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fang Yongjin; Zhang Jiexin; Ai Xinping; Cao Yuliang; Yang Hanxi; Xiao Lifen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1600392</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2198-3844</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered as a promising alternative for the next generation of electric storage systems due to their similar electrochemistry to Li-ion batteries and the low cost of sodium resources.  Exploring appropriate electrode materials with decent electrochemical performance is the key issue for development of sodium ion batteries.  Due to the high structural stability, facile reaction mechanism and rich structural diversity, phosphate framework materials have attracted increasing attention as promising electrode materials for sodium ion batteries.  Herein, we review the latest advances and progresses in the exploration of phosphate framework materials especially related to single-phosphates, pyrophosphates and mixed-phosphates.  We provide the detailed and comprehensive understanding of structure-composition-performance relationship of materials and try to show the advantages and disadvantages of the materials for use in SIBs.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Lab.-size LiNi0.8Co0.15Al0.05O2/graphite lithium-ion pouch cells were cycled over 100% depth of discharge at room temp. and 60° in order to investigate high-temp. degrdn. mechanisms of this important technol.  Capacity fade for the cell was correlated with that for the individual components, using electrochem. anal. of the electrodes and other diagnostic techniques.  The high-temp. cell lost 65% of its initial capacity after 140 cycles at 60° compared to only a 4% loss for the cell cycled at room temp.  Cell ohmic impedance increased significantly with a elevated temp. cycling, resulting in some of the capacity loss at the C/2 rate.  However, as detd. with slow rate testing of the individual electrodes, the anode retained most of its original capacity, while the cathode lost 65%, even when cycled with a fresh source of lithium.  Diagnostic evaluation of cell components using x-ray diffraction, Raman, and current-sensing at. force microscopy suggests that capacity loss occurs primarily due to a rise in the impedance of the cathode, esp. at the end of charge.  The impedance rise may be caused in part by a loss of the conductive carbon at the surface of the cathode and/or by an org. film on the surface of the cathode that becomes non-ionically conductive at low lithium content.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref63"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref63" class="refNumLink">63</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit63" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2020.227954"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bag, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Reid, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Butler, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thangadurai, V.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Studies on Symmetric Solid-State Na-Ion Full Cell Using Na3V2(PO4)3 Electrodes and Polymer Composite Electrolyte</span>. <i>J. The recent trend in developing solid-state-Li batteries also thrives on exploring the all-solid-state Na battery.  Herein, we report a solid-state Na-ion full cell working at room temp. using a polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF)-based composite polymer electrolyte and NaZr2(PO4)3-structured Na3V2(PO4)3 (NVP) electrode. Herein, the highly reversible Ca-intercalation capability of NASICON-type NaV2(PO4)3 makes it a potential cathode material for nonaq. Ca-ion batteries, with high capacity and voltage and good cyclability (90 mA h g-1 and ∼ 3.4 V at 11.7 mA g-1 and 75°; 70 mA h g-1 and ∼ 3.2 V at 5.85 mA g-1 and 25°).  Although this work shows only the capability of the cathode, not a full-cell performance, it does demonstrate exptl. that a poly-oxyanionic material can provide an outstanding host structure for Ca diffusion at room temp. with high energy-storage capability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref66"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref66" class="refNumLink">66</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit66" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.0c18670"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rudola, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Balaya, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Investigations of Thermal Stability and Solid Electrolyte Interphase on Na <sub>2</sub> Ti <sub>3</sub> O <sub>7</sub> /C as a Non-Carbonaceous Anode Material for Sodium Storage Using Non-Flammable Ether-Based Electrolyte</span>. <i>ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>13</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">10</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">11732</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">11740</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/acsami.0c18670</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D13%26publication_year%3D2021%26pages%3D11732-11740%26journal%3DACS%2BAppl.%2BMater.%2BInterfaces%26issue%3D10%26author%3DK.%2BDu%26author%3DA.%2BRudola%26author%3DP.%2BBalaya%26title%3DInvestigations%2Bof%2BThermal%2BStability%2Band%2BSolid%2BElectrolyte%2BInterphase%2Bon%2BNa%2B2%2BTi%2B3%2BO%2B7%2B%252FC%2Bas%2Ba%2BNon-Carbonaceous%2BAnode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BStorage%2BUsing%2BNon-Flammable%2BEther-Based%2BElectrolyte%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsami.0c18670&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c10189&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsami.0c18670&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref67"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref67" class="refNumLink">67</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit67" data-doi="10.1016/S0013-4686(96)00231-9"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Aurbach, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Granot, E.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The Study of Electrolyte Solutions Based on Solvents from the “Glyme” Family (Linear Polyethers) for Secondary Li Battery Systems</span>. <i>Electrochim. Acta</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1997</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>42</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">4</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">697</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">718</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/S0013-4686(96)00231-9</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D42%26publication_year%3D1997%26pages%3D697-718%26journal%3DElectrochim.%2BActa%26issue%3D4%26author%3DD.%2BAurbach%26author%3DE.%2BGranot%26title%3DThe%2BStudy%2Bof%2BElectrolyte%2BSolutions%2BBased%2Bon%2BSolvents%2Bfrom%2Bthe%2B%25E2%2580%259CGlyme%25E2%2580%259D%2BFamily%2B%2528Linear%2BPolyethers%2529%2Bfor%2BSecondary%2BLi%2BBattery%2BSystems%26doi%3D10.1016%252FS0013-4686%252896%252900231-9&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c10189&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2FS0013-4686%2896%2900231-9&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit67R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">67</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The study of electrolyte solutions based on solvents from the "glyme" family (linear polyethers) for secondary Li battery systems</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Aurbach, Doron; Granot, Eran</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochimica Acta</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1996</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">42</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">697-718</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The behavior of Li electrodes was studied in solns. of dimethoxy ethane (DME), Et glyme (EG, CH3CH2-O-CH2-OCH2CH3) and diglyme (DG, CH3-O-CH2-CH2-O-CH2CH2-OCH3).  Salts from the LiAsF6, LiClO4, LiSO3CF3, LiN(SO2CF3)2, LiBF4, LiBr and LiI list were used as electrolytes and 1-3 Dioxolane (DN), propylene, ethylene and di-Me carbonates were used as co-solvents.  The correlation amongst the surface chem., morphol. and cycling efficiency of Li electrodes in these solns. was studied using surface sensitive FTIR spectroscopy, electron microscopy (SEM) and chronopotentiometry.  Basically, solns. of the above salts in the three glyme solvents tested, as well as those tested in solvent mixts. contg. DN or alkyl carbonates with any one of the glyme solvents, do not seem suitable for use in rechargeable Li battery systems having Li metal anodes because the rough morphol. of Li upon deposition-dissoln. cycling in them leads to low Li cycling efficiency.  The key factor in this respect seems to be the surface chem. developed on Li in these solvents.  This point is discussed thoroughly in this paper.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript"         </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy d. is the main property of rechargeable batteries that has driven the entire technol. forward in past decades.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) now surpass other, previously competitive battery types (for example, lead-acid and nickel metal hydride) but still require extensive further improvement to, in particular, extend the operation hours of mobile IT devices and the driving mileages of all-elec. vehicles.  In this Review, we present a crit. overview of a wide range of post-LIB materials and systems that could have a pivotal role in meeting such demands.  We divide battery systems into two categories: near-term and long-term technologies.  To provide a realistic and balanced perspective, we describe the operating principles and remaining issues of each post-LIB technol., and also evaluate these materials under com. cell configurations.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1038/s41578-021-00324-w"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Usiskin, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Popovic, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Law, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Balaya, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maier, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Fundamentals, Status and Promise of Sodium-Based Batteries</span>. <i>Nat.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A Review.  Na-based batteries have shown substantial progress in recent years and are promising candidates for mitigating the supply risks assocd. with Li-based batteries.  In this Review, Na and Li batteries are compared in terms of fundamental principles and specific materials.  Principles for the rational design of a Na battery architecture are discussed.  Recent prototypes are surveyed to demonstrate that Na cells offer realistic alternatives that are competitive with some Li cells in terms of performance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1038/natrevmats.2018.13"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vaalma, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Weil, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Cost and Resource Analysis of Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1039/C9TA08991H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tripathi, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rudola, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gajjela, S. R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xi, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Balaya, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Developing an O3 Type Layered Oxide Cathode and Its Application in 18650 Commercial Type Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J. Mater. Chem. A</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">45</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">25944</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">25960</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/C9TA08991H</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Developing an O3 type layered oxide cathode and its application in 18650 commercial type Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Tripathi, Abhinav; Rudola, Ashish; Gajjela, Satyanarayana Reddy; Xi, Shibo; Balaya, Palani</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">45</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">25944-25960</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel, water-stable and high energy d. cathode material Na0.9Cu0.12Ni0.10Fe0.30Mn0.43Ti0.05O2 (NCNFMT) is reported here along with a thorough understanding of structural events during battery operation.  Systematic substitutions are carried out, which lead to increase in specific energy densities of this family of cathodes from 274.6 W h kgcathode-1 (NCFM - Na0.9Cu0.22Fe0.30Mn0.48O2) to 304.2 W h kgcathode-1 (NCFMT - Na0.9Cu0.22Fe0.30Mn0.43Ti0.05O2) and finally to 350.7 W h kgcathode-1 (NCNFMT - Na0.9Cu0.12Ni0.10Fe0.30Mn0.43Ti0.05O2).  Operando X-ray diffraction reveals phase transformations and ex situ EXAFS shows the evolution of local environments around transition metals during charge/discharge.  Monoclinic distortions in the NCFM material during O3-P3 phase transformations are suppressed by Ti4+ substitution leading to improvements in the cycling performance of NCFMT.  Cu-O octahedral sites exhibit huge Jahn-Teller distortion: Ni2+ substitution in place of Cu2+ not only leads to more ordered Ni-O, but it also helps ext. more Na ions from the O3 cathode structure, thus boosting the capacity while also showing good cycling stability due to the highly reversible bond-length and local environmental changes as revealed by EXAFS analyses.  XPS shows a titanium-rich surface for NCFMT and NCNFMT which helps improve water-stability.  The capacity retention after 200 cycles at 0.2C is 84%, 96% and 90% for NCFM, NCFMT and NCNFMT resp.  The delivered storage capacities of NCFM, NCFMT and NCNFMT are 21 mA h g-1, 47 mA h g-1 and 60 mA h g-1 resp. at 3C. 18650 type Na-ion batteries using the NCNFMT cathode material against a hard carbon anode are also reported to demonstrate potential scalability of the NCNFMT cathode and efficacy of a 1 M NaBF4 tetraglyme electrolyte system for the first time. 18650 cells deliver a specific energy d. of 62 W h kgtotal_18650_weight-1 with 90% energy efficiency, thus being suitable for large scale energy storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref6" class="rightTabRefNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit6" data-doi="10.1002/ange.201606508"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goodenough, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The development of advanced rechargeable batteries provides a great opportunity for basic and applied researchers to collectively overcome challenging scientific and technol. barriers that directly address a crit. need for energy storage.  In addn. to novel battery chemistries often scientifically reviewed, advanced battery structures via technol. innovations that boost battery performance are also worthy of attention.  In this context, bipolar electrodes (BEs) are capable of improving the specific power, simplifying cell components, and reducing manufg. costs for rechargeable batteries.  By focusing on the fundamentals and applications of BEs in rechargeable batteries, the rational utilization of BEs from an academic perspective is considered.  The progress and challenges of BEs are discussed and summarized in detail.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Na-ion batteries (SIBs) receive significant attention for electrochem. energy storage and conversion owing to their wide availability and the low cost of Na resources.  However, SIBs face challenges of low specific energy, short cycling life, and insufficient specific power, owing to the heavy mass and large radius of Na+ ions.  As an important component of SIBs, cathode materials have a significant effect on the SIB electrochem. performance.  The most recent advances and prospects of inorg. and org. cathode materials are summarized here.  Among current cathode materials, layered transition-metal oxides achieve high specific energies around 600 mW h g-1 owing to their high specific capacities of 180-220 mA h g-1 and their moderate operating potentials of 2.7-3.2 V (vs. Na+/Na).  Porous Na3V2(PO4)3/C nanomaterials exhibit excellent cycling performance with almost 100% retention over 1000 cycles owing to their robust structural framework.  Recent emerging cathode materials, such as amorphous NaFePO4 and pteridine derivs. show interesting electrochem. properties and attractive prospects for application in SIBs.  Future work should focus on strategies to enhance the overall performance of cathode materials in terms of specific energy, cycling life, and rate capability with cationic doping, anionic substitution, morphol. fabrication, and electrolyte matching.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref13" class="rightTabRefNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit13" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201200803"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saravanan, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mason, C.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium ion batteries are attractive for the rapidly emerging large-scale energy storage market for intermittent renewable resources.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. stability window (EW) of polyether based salt-in-polymer electrolytes was investigated using d. functional theory (DFT) calcns.  The electrolyte systems investigated consisted of polyethylene oxide (PEO) in either lithium-bis(trifluoromethanesulfonyl)imide (LiTFSI) or lithium-hexafluorophosphate (LiPF6) salt and the EW was detd. by performing calcns. of redn. and oxidn. potentials of isolated ethylene oxide (EO) oligomer and the resp. salt species in a continuum solvent.  The simulations suggest that the cathodic limit of the polymer-salt system is defined by the redn. potential of the salt anion and that both salt anions considered are unstable against Li anode.  The anodic limit is defined by EO and it is stable against most com. cathodes.  Including explicit salt mols. in the calcns. shows that the predicted EW is changed by ∼0.4V.  The calcns. further reveal that the EW is dependent on the type of the salt mol.  Perfluoropolyether, a perfluorinated analog of PEO that has lower redn. potential and higher oxidn. potential in isolation as compared to PEO, improved both oxidn. and redn. stability of the polymer-salt system.  Substitution of other functional groups to PEO improved the electrochem. stability to potentially accommodate higher voltage window requirements.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref26" class="rightTabRefNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit26" data-doi="10.1021/acsaem.3c02137"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Patra, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Matsumi, N.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Charge–Discharge Behavior of Lithium-Ion Batteries Using a Polymer Electrolyte Bearing High-Density Functional Groups</span>. <i>ACS Appl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With higher nature abundance and lower prodn. cost of sodium-based materials compared to lithium-based ones, sodium batteries have been arising as one of the most promising energy storage systems serving as complementary power sources to the popular lithium-ion batteries.  Replacing conventional liq. electrolytes contg. flammable liq. solvents with polymer electrolytes (PEs) empowers sodium batteries with inherently enhanced safety at a low expense of processability.  In this contribution, we present a comprehensive overview on the research progresses and advances in the PE-based sodium batteries, including three main categories of PEs, i.e., solid polymer electrolytes (SPEs), composite solid polymer electrolytes (CSPEs), and plasticized/gel polymer electrolytes (PPEs/GPEs).  The energy densities of sodium batteries at cell level are estd. and compared with their lithium counterparts.  The research development of PE-based sodium batteries is scrutinized and the remaining challenges and possible solns. are intensively discussed.  The purpose of the present work is not only to provide a well-balanced status quo of PE-based sodium batteries but also to address the possible remedies for improving their performance and other post lithium battery technologies, in hope of facilitating their large-scale deployment in the market.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref28"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref28" class="rightTabRefNumLink">28</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit28" data-doi="10.1021/acsaem.2c01296"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bristi, A. Poly(vinylidene fluoride), PVDF, and its copolymers are the family of polymers with the highest dielec. const. and electroactive response, including piezoelec., pyroelec. and ferroelec. effects.  The electroactive properties are increasingly important in a wide range of applications such as in biomedicine, energy generation and storage, monitoring and control, and include the development of sensors and actuators, separator and filtration membranes and smart scaffolds, among others.  For many of these applications the polymer should be in one of its electroactive phases.  This review presents the developments and summarizes the main characteristics of the electroactive phases of PVDF and copolymers, indicates the different processing strategies as well as the way in which the phase content is identified and quantified. However, reports on the anal. of α,β and γ phases often have conflicting views based on measured data. Five stages are identified and intensive information on the growth and phase transition of α and γ phases in each stage is offered.  Specifically, an induction periods obsd. for both α and γ phases in the stage I.  In stage II, the α phase is initiated while the γ phase is still in the induction period, i.e., the nucleation and development of α phase are prior to the γ phase, although the resultant sample presents exclusively pure γ phase. Incorporating more CTAB impedes the growth of α phase while accelerates the development of γ phase.  In stage IV, an α-γ phase solid transition accompanies the melt crystn. of the γ phase, and the α-γ phase transition is accelerated by CTAB mols. dramatically.  Finally, the melt crystn. of the γ phase is completed, only α-γ phase transition is obsd. DFT heat capacity calcns. using ab-initio methods for both R‾3 and Fm‾3m structures show better correlation with the Raman observations.  The differences between Raman and pXRD data in LiPF6 appear to be in common with that obsd. in other materials that are close to phase transitions at the temp. where order/disorder and phase transition processes are known to occur.  In these circumstances, Raman spectroscopy emerges as a more sensitive probe of local structural changes than pXRD, which is known to probe the overall av. long range order of cryst. materials.  The results are interpreted through the relation between the local symmetry, internal energy and the heat capacity.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref38"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref38" class="rightTabRefNumLink">38</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit38" data-doi="10.1023/A:1005186701329"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Barthel, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchner, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wismeth, E.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">FTIR Spectroscopy of Ion Solvation of LiClO4 and LiSCN in Acetonitrile, Benzonitrile, and Propylene Carbonate</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Salts of the same anions of Mg2+, Na+, and Li+ exhibit different hygroscopic properties.  These differences are attributed to the mol. structural properties of the H bonding network of the H2O mols. in the 2nd and 1st hydrated layers of Mg2+, Na+, and Li+.  To study the structures of H2O mols., in particular, the presence of H2O monomers, Raman spectra of single levitated droplets of aq. NaClO4, LiClO4, and Mg(ClO4)2 solns. from dild. concns. to high supersaturations were measured.  Because heterogeneous nucleation was suppressed in these levitated droplets, supersatd. droplets with a H2O-to-solute ratio (WSR) ≥2 was achieved.  Taking advantage of the structure breaking effect of ClO-4on the H bonding network of H2O mols., Raman spectra of H2O monomers in these highly supersatd. droplets were obsd.  At a low WSR, two peaks at 3549 and 3588 cm-1 for H2O monomers with one and two weak H bonds with ClO4- were obsd. for NaClO4 droplets.  Compared with those of the NaClO4 droplets, the peaks of the H2O monomers in supersatd. LiClO4 and Mg(ClO4)2 droplets red shifted to 3553 and 3546 cm-1, resp.  This observation is consistent with the order of the increase of the polarization effect of the cations, i.e., Na+ &lt; Li+ &lt; Mg2+.  The intensity ratios of the strongly H-bonded components to the H2O monomers, i.e., I3440/I3549 and I3289/I3549 for NaClO4, I3455/I3553 and I3275/I3553 for LiClO4, and I3411/I3546 and I3440/I3549 for Mg(ClO4)2, were used to study the effects of the presence of ClO4- on the H2O structures of the hydration layers of Na+, Li+, and Mg2+.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The use of polymer electrolytes instead of liq. org. systems is considered key for enhancing the safety of lithium batteries and may, in addn., enable the transition to high-energy lithium metal anodes.  An intrinsic limitation, however, is their rather low ionic cond. at ambient temp.  Nonetheless, it has been suggested that this might be overcome by decoupling the ion transport and the segmental relaxation of the coordinating polymer.  Here, we provide an overview of the different approaches to achieve such decoupling, including a brief recapitulation of the segmental-relaxation dependent ion conduction mechanism, exemplarily focusing on the archetype of polymer electrolytes - polyethylene oxide (PEO).  In fact, while the understanding of the underlying mechanisms has greatly improved within recent years, it remains rather challenging to outperform PEO-based electrolyte systems. Na3V2(PO4)3/C (NVP/C) is known to be a suitable electrode material for such batteries that can be used as an anode or cathode.  Here, NVP/C-based electrodes were investigated in different cell configurations.  The electrodes were cycled against the anode materials hard carbon, Sb/C, SnSb/C, and sodium metal.  Furthermore, NVP vs. NVP was investigated.  When NVP/C is cycled against the other anode materials, the cells exhibit relatively poor reliability, but in NVP-NVP cells, a high cycling stability was obsd. and more than 1500 cycles with a capacity retention of 80% were achieved. Exploring appropriate electrode materials with decent electrochemical performance is the key issue for development of sodium ion batteries.  Due to the high structural stability, facile reaction mechanism and rich structural diversity, phosphate framework materials have attracted increasing attention as promising electrode materials for sodium ion batteries.  Herein, we review the latest advances and progresses in the exploration of phosphate framework materials especially related to single-phosphates, pyrophosphates and mixed-phosphates.  We provide the detailed and comprehensive understanding of structure-composition-performance relationship of materials and try to show the advantages and disadvantages of the materials for use in SIBs.  In addition, some new perspectives about phosphate framework materials for SIBs are also discussed.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Lab.-size LiNi0.8Co0.15Al0.05O2/graphite lithium-ion pouch cells were cycled over 100% depth of discharge at room temp. and 60° in order to investigate high-temp. degrdn. mechanisms of this important technol.  Capacity fade for the cell was correlated with that for the individual components, using electrochem. anal. of the electrodes and other diagnostic techniques.  The high-temp. cell lost 65% of its initial capacity after 140 cycles at 60° compared to only a 4% loss for the cell cycled at room temp.  Cell ohmic impedance increased significantly with a elevated temp. cycling, resulting in some of the capacity loss at the C/2 rate.  However, as detd. with slow rate testing of the individual electrodes, the anode retained most of its original capacity, while the cathode lost 65%, even when cycled with a fresh source of lithium.  Diagnostic evaluation of cell components using x-ray diffraction, Raman, and current-sensing at. force microscopy suggests that capacity loss occurs primarily due to a rise in the impedance of the cathode, esp. at the end of charge.         (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">697-718</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ELCAAV</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4686</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The behavior of Li electrodes was studied in solns. of dimethoxy ethane (DME), Et glyme (EG, CH3CH2-O-CH2-OCH2CH3) and diglyme (DG, CH3-O-CH2-CH2-O-CH2CH2-OCH3).  Salts from the LiAsF6, LiClO4, LiSO3CF3, LiN(SO2CF3)2, LiBF4, LiBr and LiI list were used as electrolytes and 1-3 Dioxolane (DN), propylene, ethylene and di-Me carbonates were used as co-solvents.  The correlation amongst the surface chem., morphol. and cycling efficiency of Li electrodes in these solns. was studied using surface sensitive FTIR spectroscopy, electron microscopy (SEM) and chronopotentiometry.  Basically, solns. of the above salts in the three glyme solvents tested, as well as those tested in solvent mixts. contg. DN or alkyl carbonates with any one of the glyme solvents, do not seem suitable for use in rechargeable Li battery systems having Li metal anodes because the rough morphol. of Li upon deposition-dissoln. cycling in them leads to low Li cycling efficiency.  The key factor in this respect seems to be the surface chem. developed on Li in these solvents.  This point is discussed thoroughly in this paper.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i17">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c10189?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c10189</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Electrolyte preparation scheme; temperature-dependent EIS curves of all electrolyte investigated; elemental mapping, time dependent EIS curves, mechanical strength and thermal stability of polymer electrolyte; XRD, crystal structure, TEM, elemental mapping of Zn doped NVP; CV curves, d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plot, XPS of NVP electrode; charge–discharge curves of electrodes; quasi-solid-state battery testing; postmortem analysis of solid-state battery electrode after cycling; literature comparison table of symmetric Na-ion battery (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c10189/suppl_file/am4c10189_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c10189/suppl_file/am4c10189_si_001.pdf">am4c10189_si_001.pdf (2.2 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

        <script nonce="9318851c1ad154b8-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>
